بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة فإن نفس الوجوب لا ينفك عنه وجوب الأداء؛ فيكونان معا بعد الحنث، ونحن نقول: هذا الفرق ساقط؛ لأنّ ذات المال إنّما تقصد في حقوق العباد، وأما في حقوق الله تعالى فالمقصود هو الأداء؛ فيكون كالبدني، لا ينفك فيه نفس الوجوب عن وجوب الأداء. أي المال وعندنا التعليق بالشرط لا ينعقد سببًا حقيقة وإن انعقد صورةً، فإذا قال: "إن دخلت الدار فأنتِ طالق" فكأنه لم يتكلّم بقوله: "أنت طالق" قبل دخول الدار، فحين يوجد دخول الدار يوجد التكلّم بقوله: "أنت طالق". لأنّ الإيجاب لا يوجد إلّا بركنه، ولا يثبت إلا في محله، وههنا وإن وجد الركن وهو "أنت طالق" لكن لم يوجد المحلّ؛ لأنّ الشرط حَالَ بينه وبين المحل فيبقى غير الإيجاب مضاف إليه أي غير متصل بالمحل، وبدون الاتصال بالمحل لا ينعقد سببًا. فيكونان أي نفس الوجوب في البدني ووجوب الأداء. (القمر) إنّما تقصد إلخ : فإنّ المقصود حصول ما ينتفع به العبد أو يدفع به الخسران وذلك يكون بالمال. (القمر) فالمقصود هو الأداء لأن المال في نفسه ليس بعبادة، إنما العبادة فعل يفعله العبد على خلاف هوى النفس طلبًا لرضوان الله تعالى بإذنه. (القمر) لا ينفك إلخ : ووجوب أداء الكفّارة بالحنث ؛ فلا يصح أداء أية كفّارة كانت قبل الحنث. (القمر) لا ينعقد سببًا : فالشرط معدوم للسببية أصالة وقصدًا وأمّا في منع الحكم فأثره بالتبع (القمر) دخول الدار: أي الذي هو الشرط. (القمر) لا يوجد إلا بركنه لأنّ الإيجاب يقوم بالركن، وهو أن يكون صادرًا من أهله. (القمر) ولا يثبت أي الركن أو الإيجاب إلّا في محلّه ؛ ولذا يكون بيع الحرّ باطلاً لعدم المحل وإن وجد الإيجاب، فإنّ محلّ البيع المالُ المتقوّم، والحرّ ليس بمال، فتأمل.(القمر) لأن الشرط حال بينه إلخ نظيره من الحسّيات الرمي؛ فإنّ نفس الرمي ليس بقتل ولكنه يعرض أن يصير قتلا إذا اتصل لسهم بالمحلّ، وإذا حال بينه وبين المرمي إليه تُرس منع الرمي من للقتل؛ لأنه منع القتل مع وجود فكذا التعليق بالشرط في الشرعيات كتاب التحقيق. (السنبلي) أي غير متصل إلخ: لما كان توهم أن كلام المصنف له غير منتظم فإنّ الواجب عليه أن يقول: فيبقى غير مضاف إليه أي إلى الحلّ وبدون الإضافة إلى المحل لا ينعقد سببًا. أو يقول: فيبقى غير متصل بالمحل وبدون الاتصال أي اتصال بالمحلّ لا ينعقد سببًا؟ دفعه الشارح بقوله: أي غير متصل إلخ. وحاصله أن المراد بالإضافة في كلام المصنف له الاتصال لا النسبة؛ فانتظم الكلام. ثم اعلم أن المراد بالاتصال كون الإيجاب مفضيًا إلى ثبوت أثره في المحل، والشرط مانع من هذا الاقتضاء. سببه انعقاده علة فإذا كان كذلك انعكس حال ،التفريعات، فيصح تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك فيما إذا قال: إن نكحتك فأنت طالق، أو إن ملكتك فأنتَ حرّ ؛ لأنه لم يوجد قوله: أنتِ طالق وأنتَ أي وقت التعليق حرّ حتى يحتاج إلى المحلّ. فإذا وجد النكاح والملك فحينئذ يكون محلا؛ لورود قوله: "أنت طالق وأنت حرّ، فلا بأس به لوقوعه في محله. وبطل التكفير بالمال قبل الحنث؛ لأنّ اليمين لا ينعقد إلا للبرّ فكيف يكون سببًا للحنث ؛ فلا يصح التقديم على السبب. وصح أن الحكم عندنا ليس لعدم الشرط، بل لعدم السبب؛ فلا يكون عدما شرعيا بل عدما أصليًّا لا يعدّى إلى غيره. وهذا هو ثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبينه وإلّا فلا يخفى أنّ قبل دخول الدار في قوله: "أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار" لو طلّق بطلاق آخر، يقع بالاتفاق بيننا وبينه؛ فتقرّر أنّ الشرط في التعليقات يدخل في السبب والحكم جميعًا؛ لأنها من قبيل الإسقاطات، أي التعليقات فإذا كان كذلك إلخ أي إذا لم ينعقد السبب سببًا في الحال وقت التعليق بالشرط. النكاح والملك أي اللذان هما الشرطان فلا يصح التقديم إلخ : أي لا يصح أداء الواجب قبل سببه فلا يصح تقديم الكفّارة بالمال على الحنث؛ فإن الحنث سببها؛ فإنه مفض إليها. بل لعدم السبب إلخ: أي عدم الحكم لعدم السبب فيكون عدما أصليًا. وعند الشافعي الله لعدم الشرط فيكون عدما شرعيا؛ فثمرة الخلاف هو العدم الأصلي، فلا يتعدي إلى غيره، والعدم الشرعي فيتعدّى إلى غيره. ولو لم يكن ثمرة الخلاف هذا، بل كان عند الشافعي بمعنى أن عدم الحكم ثابت من الشرع للزم أن لا يقع الطلاق بعد التطليق. (السنبلي) لا يعدّى إلى غيره أي بالقياس وعند الشافعي له يعدّى إلى غيره وتفصيل هذه التعدية سيجيء في البحث الثالث الآتي من الوجوه الفاسدة وهذا أي كون عدم الحكم بعدم الشرط عدما شرعيا عنده، وعدما أصليا عندنا هو ثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي له، وإلا فلا خلاف؛ لأنّ الكلّ منا ومنهم متفقون على وجود المشروط بوجود الشرط، وعلى أنّ المعلّق بالشرط معدوم قبل وجود الشرط. فلو قال: "أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار" لا يقع الطلاق في الحال قبل الدخول. ولو طلّق قبل الدخول طلاقًا آخر يقع بالاتفاق لوجود المحلّ. في التعليقات أي في التي تقبل التعليق بالشرط والخطر كالطلاق والعتاق. قبيل الإسقاطات إلخ: اعلم أنّ الطلاق والعتاق من الإسقاطات، وكلّها يصلح التعليق، فإذا دخل عليها يمنع السبب والحكم جميعًا. وأصل التعليق وكماله أن يدخل على السبب والحكم كليهما، ولا مانع ههنا، أما البيع فإنّه من الإثباتات وهي لا تقبل التعليق لا لأنه يكون معنى القمار ، فبناء على هذا كان ينبغي أن لا : يصح خيار
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. Indeed, the essence of obligation is not separable from the obligation of performance; so both are together after the violation (of the oath). And we say: this distinction is invalid, because the essence of wealth is intended in the rights of people, but in the rights of Allah Almighty, what is intended is performance; so it resembles physical (obligation), in which the essence of obligation is not separable from the obligation of performance. That is, wealth. And according to us, conditional suspension by a condition does not take effect as a cause in reality, even if it takes effect in form. So if he says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' it is as if he did not utter his statement 'you are divorced' before entering the house. So when entry into the house occurs, the utterance of his statement 'you are divorced' occurs. Because the creation of obligation only exists with its pillar, and it is only established in its locus. Here, although the pillar exists, namely 'you are divorced,' the locus does not exist, because the condition interposes between it and the locus, so the creation of obligation remains unconnected to the locus, and without connection to the locus, it does not take effect as a cause. So both (the essence of obligation in physical and the obligation of performance). (Al-Qamar) 'It is only intended' etc.: For what is intended is the attainment of benefit for the servant or the repulsion of harm, and that is through wealth. (Al-Qamar) So what is intended is performance, because wealth in itself is not worship; worship is an act performed by the servant contrary to desire, seeking the pleasure of Allah Almighty by His permission. (Al-Qamar) 'Not separable' etc.: The obligation to perform expiation upon violation; thus it is not valid to perform any expiation before violation. (Al-Qamar) 'Does not take effect as a cause': So the condition is non-existent for causality originally and intentionally, but regarding the prevention of the ruling, its effect is subsidiary. (Al-Qamar) 'Entry into the house': i.e., that which is the condition. (Al-Qamar) 'Only exists with its pillar': Because the creation of obligation is established by the pillar, which is that it emanates from its qualified person. (Al-Qamar) 'And it is only established' i.e., the pillar or the creation of obligation, except in its locus; hence the sale of a free person is invalid due to lack of locus, even if the creation of obligation exists, for the locus of sale is valuable property, and a free person is not property, so ponder. (Al-Qamar) 'Because the condition interposes between it etc.' Its analogue from the physical is shooting; for the act of shooting itself is not killing, but it becomes killing if the arrow connects with the locus, and if a shield interposes between it and the target, it prevents the shooting from becoming killing; because it prevents killing even though (the act) exists. Likewise, conditional suspension by a condition in religious matters. (Al-Sunbuli) 'i.e., unconnected etc.': Because it might be imagined that the speech of the author is unorganized, for he should have said: 'so it remains unconnected to the locus, and without connection to the locus, it does not take effect as a cause.' The commentator refuted it by his saying: 'i.e., unconnected etc.' The summary is that what is meant by addition in the author's speech is connection, not attribution; thus the speech becomes organized. Then know that what is meant by connection is that the creation of obligation leads to the establishment of its effect in the locus, and the condition prevents this efficacy. Its cause is its being a cause. When that is the case, the condition of the branches is reversed: it is valid to suspend divorce and manumission upon ownership in the case where he says, 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or 'If I own you, you are free'; because his statement 'you are divorced' and 'you are free' does not exist at the time of suspension so as to require the locus. When marriage and ownership occur, then at that time there is a locus, due to the occurrence of his statement 'you are divorced' and 'you are free', so there is no problem because it occurs in its locus. And expiation with wealth before violation is invalid, because an oath is only taken for righteousness, so how can it be a cause for violation? Thus, performance before the cause is not valid. And it is correct that the ruling according to us is not due to the absence of a condition, but due to the absence of a cause; so it is not a legal non-existence but an original non-existence that does not extend to other rulings. This is the fruit of the disagreement between us and him. Otherwise, it is not hidden that before entry into the house in his saying 'you are divorced if you enter the house,' if he divorced with another divorce, it occurs by agreement between us and him. So it is established that the condition in suspensions enters into both the cause and the ruling; because they are of the category of removals (isqatat). That is, suspensions. 'When that is the case etc.' i.e., when the cause does not take effect as a cause at the time of suspension by the condition. 'Marriage and ownership' i.e., those two that are the conditions. 'So performance before is not valid etc.' i.e., it is not valid to perform an obligation before its cause, so it is not valid to perform expiation with wealth before violation; for violation is its cause, as it leads to it. 'But due to absence of cause etc.' i.e., the absence of ruling is due to absence of cause, so it is an original non-existence. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is due to absence of condition, so it is a legal non-existence. So the fruit of the disagreement is the original non-existence, which does not extend to other rulings, whereas the legal non-existence extends to other rulings. If this were not the fruit of the disagreement, and it were according to al-Shafi'i in the sense that the absence of ruling is established by the Law, then it would necessitate that divorce after the pronouncement would not occur. (Al-Sunbuli) 'Does not extend to other rulings' i.e., by analogy, and according to al-Shafi'i, it extends to other rulings. The detail of this extension will come in the third investigation of the invalid aspects. And this, i.e., that the absence of ruling due to absence of condition is legal non-existence according to him, and original non-existence according to us, is the fruit of the disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i. Otherwise, there is no disagreement; because all of us and them agree on the existence of the conditioned upon existence of the condition, and that what is suspended on a condition is non-existent before the existence of the condition. So if he says, 'you are divorced if you enter the house,' divorce does not occur immediately before entry. And if he divorces before entry with another divorce, it occurs by agreement due to existence of the locus. 'In suspensions' i.e., in those that admit suspension by condition and risk, like divorce and manumission. 'Of the category of removals etc.': Know that divorce and manumission are from removals, and all of them admit suspension. So if it enters upon them, it prevents both the cause and the ruling. The principle of suspension and its perfection is that it enters upon both the cause and the ruling, and there is no impediment here. As for sale, it is from affirmations, and they do not admit suspension, not because it would be gambling, but according to this, it would be necessary that option is not valid.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the nature of conditional suspension (ta'liq). The author first invalidates a distinction made by some regarding financial obligations: that the essence of obligation (nafs al-wujub) is separable from the obligation of performance (wujub al-ada') in financial rights, unlike physical obligations. The author argues this distinction fails because in rights of Allah, the goal is performance itself, similar to physical acts. The core of the passage then focuses on ta'liq. The Hanafi view is that a condition (shart) in a suspended pronouncement (e.g., 'if you enter, you are divorced') prevents the cause (sabab) from being realized at the time of utterance. The condition interposes between the cause and its locus (mahall), so the cause does not take effect until the condition is fulfilled. This is analogous to shooting an arrow: the act of shooting is not killing until the arrow reaches the target. If a shield intervenes, the killing does not occur. According to Hanafis, the condition nullifies the cause itself, so the absence of the ruling is an original non-existence ('adam asli) that does not extend to other rulings. The Shafi'i position, as presented, is that the condition merely blocks the effect of the cause, making the absence a legal non-existence ('adam shar'i) that can extend to other rulings. The practical fruit (thamara) of this disagreement is shown: if a man says to his wife 'you are divorced if you enter the house' and before entry he pronounces another divorce, the second divorce occurs by agreement because the locus (the wife) exists. However, the Hanafi view that the first suspension does not create a cause prevents that suspension from affecting the second divorce, while the Shafi'i view might lead to complications if the absence were considered legal. The passage also explains that ta'liq is permissible for acts of removal (isqatat) like divorce and manumission, but not for acts of establishment (ithbatat) like sale, because those do not admit suspension. The author clarifies that the condition in suspensions enters into both the cause and the ruling, preventing both simultaneously.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional suspension of a legal effect upon the occurrence of a future event.
سبب (sabab): The cause that brings about a legal ruling; in ta'liq, the pronouncement (e.g., 'you are divorced') is the cause.
عدم أصلي ('adam asli): Original non-existence; the cause never came into being, so its absence cannot be analogically extended.
عدم شرعي ('adam shar'i): Legal non-existence; the ruling is absent by divine decree, and its absence can be analogically extended to other cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a second divorce pronounced before the condition of a suspended divorce (e.g., 'If you enter, you are divorced') still valid?
A) Because the condition only prevents the effect, not the cause.
B) Because the first suspension does not create a valid cause (sabab) at the time of utterance, so the second divorce occurs on a valid locus.
C) Because the second divorce is not subject to the condition.
D) Because the Hanafi school does not recognize the validity of conditional divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on ta'liq: a condition nullifies the cause itself, making the absence original ('adam asli) and non-extendable, which differs from the Shafi'i view of a legal absence ('adam shar'i).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
The Nature of Non-Existence in Ta'liq: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Usul dispute on nature of non-existence in ta'liq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between original non-existence (adam asli) and legal non-existence (adam shar'i) in conditional suspensions, and apply the Hanafi view that absence of a condition does not extend to other rulings.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, absence of a condition is original non-existence that does not extend by analogy.

This passage from Noor al-Anwar examines the invalid aspects of ta'liq and the theoretical disagreement on whether the non-existence of a ruling when a condition is absent is original or legal. The Hanafi school holds it is original (adam asli), meaning it does not extend to other cases by qiyas, while the Shafi'i school views it as legal (adam shar'i) that does extend. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving consistent rulings in conditional acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the examples and the rule
First, note the examples of valid ta'liq (divorce on marriage, emancipation on ownership) and the invalidity of paying kaffarah before hanth. The text establishes that ta'liq is valid in isqatat (waivers) like divorce and emancipation, where the condition enters both cause and ruling.
Isqatat are acts that remove a right or property; ithbatat are acts that establish a right.
2
Locate the core dispute
The text states: 'al-hukm ‘indana laysa li‘adam al-shart bal li‘adam al-sabab' – according to us (Hanafis), the ruling is absent due to absence of the cause, not the condition. This is the central point of disagreement with al-Shafi'i.
The phrase 'indana refers to the Hanafi position; 'indahu refers to al-Shafi'i.
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
The proof is the example of a second divorce before entry: if the non-existence were legal, it would prevent the second divorce, but it is agreed that the second divorce takes effect. This shows the non-existence is original.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on consensus (ittifaq).
4
Understand the Shafi'i counter-argument
The Shafi'i view treats the condition as a restriction (qayd) for the consequence, which implies negation when the condition is absent (mafhum al-mukhalafa). The text discusses the linguistic basis for this view.
The author notes that grammarians do not necessarily hold this view; it is attributed to al-Sakkaki in al-Miftah.
5
Evaluate the practical difference (thamara)
The fruit of the disagreement is whether the non-existence extends to other cases by qiyas. Hanafis say it does not; Shafi'is say it does. The text promises further detail in a later section.
This affects how new cases are analogized from the original conditional ruling.

Source Text

بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة فإذا كان كذلك انعكس حال التفريعات، فيصح تعليق الطلاق والعتاق بالملك فيما إذا قال: إن نكحتك فأنت طالق، أو إن ملكتك فأنتَ حرّ ؛ لأنه لم يوجد قوله: أنتِ طالق وأنتَ أي وقت التعليق حرّ حتى يحتاج إلى المحلّ. فإذا وجد النكاح والملك فحينئذ يكون محلا؛ لورود قوله: "أنت طالق وأنت حرّ، فلا بأس به لوقوعه في محله. وبطل التكفير بالمال قبل الحنث؛ لأنّ اليمين لا ينعقد إلا للبرّ فكيف يكون سببًا للحنث ؛ فلا يصح التقديم على السبب. وصح أن الحكم عندنا ليس لعدم الشرط، بل لعدم السبب؛ فلا يكون عدما شرعيا بل عدما أصليًّا لا يعدّى إلى غيره. وهذا هو ثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبينه وإلّا فلا يخفى أنّ قبل دخول الدار في قوله: "أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار" لو طلّق بطلاق آخر، يقع بالاتفاق بيننا وبينه؛ فتقرّر أنّ الشرط في التعليقات يدخل في السبب والحكم جميعًا؛ لأنها من قبيل الإسقاطات، أي التعليقات فإذا كان كذلك إلخ أي إذا لم ينعقد السبب سببًا في الحال وقت التعليق بالشرط. النكاح والملك أي اللذان هما الشرطان فلا يصح التقديم إلخ : أي لا يصح أداء الواجب قبل سببه فلا يصح تقديم الكفّارة بالمال على الحنث؛ فإن الحنث سببها؛ فإنه مفض إليها. بل لعدم السبب إلخ: أي عدم الحكم لعدم السبب فيكون عدما أصليًا. وعند الشافعي الله لعدم الشرط فيكون عدما شرعيا؛ فثمرة الخلاف هو العدم الأصلي، فلا يتعدي إلى غيره، والعدم الشرعي فيتعدّى إلى غيره. ولو لم يكن ثمرة الخلاف هذا، بل كان عند الشافعي بمعنى أن عدم الحكم ثابت من الشرع للزم أن لا يقع الطلاق بعد التطليق. (السنبلي) لا يعدّى إلى غيره أي بالقياس وعند الشافعي له يعدّى إلى غيره وتفصيل هذه التعدية سيجيء في البحث الثالث الآتي من الوجوه الفاسدة وهذا أي كون عدم الحكم بعدم الشرط عدما شرعيًّا عنده، وعدما أصليًّا عندنا هو ثمرة الخلاف بيننا وبين الشافعي له، وإلا فلا خلاف؛ لأنّ الكلّ منا ومنهم متفقون على وجود المشروط بوجود الشرط، وعلى أنّ المعلّق بالشرط معدوم قبل وجود الشرط. فلو قال: "أنت طالق إن دخلت الدار" لا يقع الطلاق في الحال قبل الدخول. ولو طلّق قبل الدخول طلاقًا آخر يقع بالاتفاق لوجود المحلّ. في التعليقات أي في التي تقبل التعليق بالشرط والخطر كالطلاق والعتاق. قبيل الإسقاطات إلخ: اعلم أنّ الطلاق والعتاق من الإسقاطات، وكلّها يصلح التعليق، فإذا دخل عليها يمنع السبب والحكم جميعًا. وأصل التعليق وكماله أن يدخل على السبب والحكم كليهما، ولا مانع ههنا، أما البيع فإنّه من الإثباتات وهي لا تقبل التعليق لا لأنه يكون معنى القمار ، فبناء على هذا كان ينبغي أن لا يصح خيار الشرط فيه، إلا أنه شرع على خلاف القياس للضرورة؛ فيكون داخلاً في الحكم فقط دون السبب، لارتفاع الضرورة حينئذ، والضرورة تقدّر بقدرها؛ ولأنه لو دخل في السبب والحكم جميعًا، لبطل معنى الإثبات ورعايته بالكلية، ولو لم يدخل فيهما لم يبق أثر الشرط أصلاً؛ فصرنا إلى ما ذكرنا رعاية للجانبين.(السنبلي) التعليق بكماله : أي التعليق الكامل، وهو تعليق السبب والحكم جميعًا. (القمر) من قبيل الإثباتات: فإنه يثبت الملك. إذ به: أي بالتعليق والخطر والشرط يصير البيع قمارًا وهو حرام (القمر) مانعا للحكم: فقط فإنّ القياس أن لا يجوز البيع مع خيار الشرط كما لا يجوز بشروط أخر، إلّا أنّ الشرع جوّز ذلك ضرورةً دفع الغبن؛ فيتقدّر بقدر الضرورة، وهي تندفع بجعل الشرط مانعا لحكم البيع وهو الملك دون السبب وهو البيع؛ لئلا يلغو الشرط ويقل الخطر مع حصول المقصود وهو دفع الغبن؛ فإنه يمكن لصاحب الخيار فسخ البيع. (القمر) دون السبب ولذا إذا حلف لا يبيع، فباع بشرط الخيار يحنث؛ لأنّ شرط الخيار ليس بمانع للسبب؛ فيتحقق البيع. (القمر) وقد يقرّر إلخ : المقرّر صاحب "التلويح" . (القمر) قيد له أي بمنزلة الظرف أو الحال.(القمر) يفيد حصر إلخ فالقيد مخصص، فيلزم نفي الحكم عند عدم هذا القيد أي الشرط. (القمر) مذهب أهل العربية قيل: إنّ هذه النسبة افتراء، فإنّ أهل العربية قالوا: إن الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء، فالمجموع كلام وليس أحد من طرفيه كلامًا، ولم يقولوا إنّ الكلام هو الجزاء، والشرط قيد له، بل إنما قاله صاحب "المفتاح". (القمر) وساكت إلخ : أقول: للخصم أن يقول: إنا سلّمنا أنّ الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء، فالمجموع كلام مفيد لحكم تعليقي بالمنطوق. لكنه لا نسلم أنه ساكت عن سائر التقادير، بل هو عين النزاع؛ فإنا نقول: إنه يدلّ على نفي الحكم عند عدم الشرط بطريق مفهوم المخالفة. (القمر) وهو أي الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء. (القمر) الجواب: جواب عنه أي عن الوصف، لأن الشافعي له ألحق الوصف بالشرط. (القمر)
Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: Chapter on mentioning the invalid aspects. When it is thus, the state of the branches is reversed. Thus, suspending divorce and emancipation upon ownership is valid when he says: 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or 'If I own you, you are free', because his statement 'You are divorced' or 'You are free' at the time of suspension does not exist such that it requires a locus. When marriage and ownership occur, then at that time it becomes a locus, because of the occurrence of his statement 'You are divorced' and 'You are free'. So there is no harm because it occurs in its locus. And expiation with property before the violation is invalid, because the oath is only contracted for piety, so how can it be a cause for violation? Thus advancement on the cause is not valid. And it is correct that according to us the ruling is not absent due to absence of the condition, but due to absence of the cause; so it is not a legal non-existence but an original non-existence that does not extend to others. This is the fruit of the disagreement between us and him. Otherwise, it is not hidden that before entering the house in his statement 'You are divorced if you enter the house', if he divorces with another divorce, it occurs by consensus between us and him. So it is established that the condition in suspensions enters into both the cause and the ruling, because they are of the category of waivers. (Commentary): 'i.e. the suspensions' 'When it is thus etc.' means when the cause does not become a cause at the moment of suspension by the condition. 'Marriage and ownership' i.e. which are the two conditions. 'So advancement is not valid etc.' i.e. it is not valid to perform an obligation before its cause, so it is not valid to present the expiation with property before the violation, for the violation is its cause because it leads to it. 'But due to absence of cause etc.' i.e. the absence of the ruling is due to absence of the cause, so it is an original non-existence. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it is due to absence of the condition, so it is a legal non-existence. Thus the fruit of the disagreement is the original non-existence, which does not extend to others, and the legal non-existence extends to others. If there were no fruit of this disagreement, but rather according to al-Shafi'i it meant that the absence of the ruling is established from the Law, then it would necessitate that the divorce would not occur after the divorce. (al-Sanbali) 'Does not extend to others' i.e. by analogy, and according to al-Shafi'i it extends to others, and the detail of this extension will come in the third investigation following from the invalid aspects. And this, that the absence of the ruling due to absence of the condition is a legal non-existence according to him and an original non-existence according to us, is the fruit of the disagreement between us and al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. Otherwise, there is no disagreement, because all of us and them agree on the existence of the conditioned upon existence of the condition, and that the suspended-with-condition is non-existent before the existence of the condition. So if he says: 'You are divorced if you enter the house', the divorce does not occur immediately before entry. And if he divorces before entry with another divorce, it occurs by consensus due to existence of the locus. 'In suspensions' i.e. in those that accept suspension by a condition and risk, like divorce and emancipation. 'Category of waivers etc.': Know that divorce and emancipation are from waivers, and all of them are fit for suspension. So when it enters upon them, it prevents both the cause and the ruling. The origin and perfection of suspension is that it enters upon both the cause and the ruling, and there is no hindrance here. As for sale, it is from establishments, and they do not accept suspension, because it becomes a form of gambling. Based on this, it would be necessary that the option of condition is not valid in it, except that it has been legislated contrary to analogy due to necessity; so it enters into the ruling only, not the cause, because the necessity at that time is removed, and necessity is estimated according to its measure; and because if it entered into both the cause and the ruling, the meaning of establishment and its observance would be nullified entirely, and if it did not enter into them, no trace of the condition would remain at all; so we have adopted what we mentioned to observe both aspects. (al-Sanbali) 'Complete suspension' i.e. the complete suspension, which is suspending both the cause and the ruling. (al-Qamar) 'From establishments' i.e. it establishes ownership. 'By it' i.e. by suspension, risk, and condition, sale becomes gambling, which is forbidden. (al-Qamar) 'Preventing the ruling only' i.e. the analogy is that sale with the option of condition is not permissible just as it is not permissible with other conditions, except that the Law allowed it by necessity to prevent loss; so it is estimated according to the need for necessity, and the necessity is repelled by making the condition a preventer of the ruling of sale, which is ownership, not the cause, which is the sale; so that the condition is not nullified and the risk is reduced while achieving the objective, which is preventing loss; for the owner of the option can rescind the sale. (al-Qamar) 'Not the cause', and therefore if he swears not to sell, and then sells with an option condition, he violates his oath, because the option condition does not prevent the cause; so the sale takes place. (al-Qamar) 'And it may be formulated etc.': The formulator is the author of al-Talwih. (al-Qamar) 'A restriction for it' i.e. in the manner of a circumstance or state. (al-Qamar) 'Conveys restriction etc.': So the restriction is a specifier, so it necessitates negation of the ruling when this restriction (i.e. the condition) is absent. (al-Qamar) 'The school of the grammarians' – it is said that this attribution is a fabrication, for the grammarians said that the ruling is between the condition and the consequence, so the totality is a speech, and neither of its two parts is a speech, and they did not say that the speech is the consequence and the condition is a restriction for it. Rather, the author of al-Miftah said that. (al-Qamar) 'And is silent etc.': I say: The opponent could say: we concede that the ruling is between the condition and the consequence, so the totality is a speech that conveys a conditional ruling explicitly. But we do not concede that it is silent about other times; rather, this is the very point of contention; for we say that it indicates the negation of the ruling when the condition is absent by way of the concept of opposition. (al-Qamar) 'It' i.e. the ruling is between the condition and the consequence. (al-Qamar) 'The answer' i.e. an answer about the description, because al-Shafi'i attached the description to the condition. (al-Qamar)
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the invalid aspects of ta'liq (suspension of rulings on conditions). The author begins by stating the rule: suspending divorce on marriage or emancipation on ownership is valid because the statement of divorce/freedom does not exist at the time of suspension; when the condition (marriage/ownership) occurs, the locus exists and the ruling applies. Then it discusses kaffarah: expiation cannot be paid before the oath is broken because the oath is meant for piety, and paying before would make the violation a cause for expiation, which is contradictory. This leads to the core theoretical dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is: when a condition is absent, is the non-existence of the ruling due to absence of the condition (shar'i non-existence) or absence of the cause (asli non-existence)? The Hanafi position is that it is asli (original) non-existence, meaning it does not extend to other rulings by analogy. The Shafi'i position is that it is shar'i (legal) non-existence, meaning it does extend. The fruit of this dispute is illustrated with the example: if a man says 'You are divorced if you enter the house', and then before entry he pronounces a separate divorce, that divorce takes effect by consensus. According to the Shafi'i view, if the non-existence were legal, it might have prevented the later divorce, but it does not. The text then explains the nature of ta'liq: it belongs to isqatat (waivers) like divorce and emancipation, which accept full ta'liq (both cause and ruling). Sales belong to ithbatat (establishments), which do not accept full ta'liq because it would lead to gambling (qimar). However, the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) in sale is allowed by necessity, but only enters the ruling, not the cause, to balance the need for flexibility with the integrity of the contract. Finally, the text discusses the linguistic analysis of conditional sentences. The Shafi'i view (ascribed to grammarians) treats the apodosis (jaza) as the speech and the condition as a restriction, implying negation of the ruling when the condition is absent. The Hanafi view (ascribed to rationalists) treats the entire conditional as one speech that conveys a ruling at the time of the condition and is silent about other times, so it does not imply negation. The text also notes that this linguistic attribution is disputed, as grammarians actually consider the ruling between condition and consequence.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): تعليق الحكم على شرط، وهو يصح في الإسقاطات كالطلاق والعتاق، ويدخل في السبب والحكم معًا
عدم أصلي (adam asli): عدم الحكم لعدم سببه، وهو عدم حقيقي لا يتعدى إلى غيره ولا يعتبر شرعيًا
عدم شرعي (adam shar'i): عدم الحكم لعدم شرطه، وهو عدم اعتباري يتعدى إلى غيره بالقياس
إسقاطات (isqatat): الأحكام التي ترفع الملك أو الحق كالطلاق والعتاق، وتقبل التعليق الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in the statement 'You are divorced if you enter the house', when the husband pronounces a separate divorce before entry, what is the ruling?
A) The second divorce does not take effect because the first conditional divorce's non-existence is legal.
B) The second divorce takes effect by consensus because the non-existence is original, not legal.
C) The second divorce takes effect only if the husband revokes the conditional divorce.
D) Neither divorce takes effect until entry occurs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the absence of a condition in ta'liq creates original non-existence (adam asli) that does not extend by analogy, a key distinction from the Shafi'i view.

Ta'liq in Divorce and Sale: The Hanafi Position
Fiqh ruling on ta'liq in divorce and sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ta'liq in divorce and sale, and explain why khiyar al-shart only prevents the ruling (ownership) and not the cause (contract).

Core Idea
Divorce accepts full suspension; sale does not—only the ruling is blocked, not the contract.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi law: why a conditional divorce (ta'liq) is valid and suspends both cause and ruling, while a conditional sale is invalid unless the condition is a valid option (khiyar al-shart) that only delays ownership. The ruling is based on the principle that sale is an act of establishing property and cannot be gambling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: ta'liq (conditional suspension) in divorce and in sale. Note that divorce accepts complete suspension (ta'liq kamil), while sale does not—a conditional sale without a valid option is invalid (gambling).
The text says 'la yaqbal al-ta'liq' for sale.
2
Understand the rationale
Why does sale not accept ta'liq? Because it is an act of establishing property (ithbat al-milk), and suspending it on a condition makes it like gambling (qimar), which is haram.
Qiyas: any uncertainty that leads to gambling is prohibited.
3
Recognize the exception: khiyar al-shart
When a valid option of condition (khiyar al-shart) is included, the sale becomes valid, but the option only prevents the ruling (ownership) from taking effect immediately, not the contract itself (the cause).
This is an exception based on necessity (darura) to prevent disadvantage (ghubn).
4
Note the fiqh consequence
If a person sells an item with a khiyar al-shart, the sale contract is valid (sahih), but ownership does not transfer until the option period ends without rescission. This allows the buyer to inspect and return the item.
Example: if one swears not to sell, then sells with khiyar al-shart, the oath is broken because the cause (sale) is present.

Statement of the divisions of the fourth division: Section on mentioning the corrupt aspects. It accepts complete suspension (ta'liq kamil) unlike sale; for sale is of the type of property establishment and does not accept suspension; because by it it becomes gambling. When the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) enters it, it prevents only the ruling, not the cause, so that the effect of the condition is minimal as much as possible. And the difference between us and him (al-Shafi'i) is established under another heading, namely that al-Shafi'i says: The speech is the consequence (jaza), and the condition is a restriction (qayd) upon it, as if he said: 'You are divorced at the time of your entering the house.' So this restriction implies the restriction of divorce to it, and this is the school of the people of grammar. And Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, says: The consequence and both (the condition and consequence) are like one single speech indicating the occurrence of divorce at the condition, and silent about other estimations, so it does not indicate restriction, and this is the school of rationalists. And the author did not mention an answer regarding the descriptive phrase (wasf), either because the answer about the condition is an answer about it, or due to its clarity and fame. ... (commentary continues)...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is a passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work discussing a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the effect of conditional clauses (ta'liq) and the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) in contracts like sale and divorce. The author distinguishes between divorce and sale: divorce accepts complete suspension (both cause and ruling are suspended), while sale does not because it would become gambling. However, when khiyar al-shart enters a sale, it only prevents the ruling (i.e., ownership) but not the cause (the contract itself), due to necessity (darura) to prevent disadvantage (ghubn). This is an exception to qiyas. Then the commentator explains the difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i on the semantics of conditional sentences. Al-Shafi'i considers the consequence (jaza) as the main speech and the condition as a restriction, implying that the ruling is confined to the time of the condition. Abu Hanifa treats the entire conditional sentence as a single utterance that is silent about other times, thus it does not imply restriction. The commentator adds that some attribute the Shafi'i view to the Arabic grammarians, but this is disputed. The Hanafi position is that the conditional does not carry a concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa) that negates the ruling when the condition is absent. This is a fundamental usul dispute rooted in language and logic.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a legal ruling on a future condition; here it refers specifically to suspending divorce on a condition, where both the cause and ruling are suspended (ta'liq kamil).
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option in a contract that allows one party to rescind the contract within a specified period; in a sale, it prevents the ruling (ownership) from taking effect immediately but does not affect the cause (the contract).
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from an established analogy (qiyas) in favor of a ruling that is considered more appropriate due to necessity, custom, or other considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, when a sale contract includes khiyar al-shart (option of condition), what is the effect on the contract?
A) The sale is invalid because it becomes gambling.
B) The sale contract (cause) is prevented from taking effect, and ownership (ruling) is also prevented.
C) The sale contract (cause) is valid and effective, but the transfer of ownership (ruling) is delayed or prevented until the option expires.
D) The condition has no effect on either the cause or the ruling.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, khiyar al-shart prevents only the ruling (ownership) from taking effect, not the cause (contract), preserving the validity of sale while allowing rescission.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Conditional Sentences: Do They Imply Restriction?
Usul dispute on conditional sentences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on whether a conditional clause implies restriction, and evaluate the linguistic and rational arguments.

Core Idea
The Hanafis hold that a conditional sentence is a single unit; it does not imply negation when the condition is absent.

This lesson examines a fundamental usul dispute: when someone says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' does it mean divorce only occurs upon entry? Imam al-Shafi'i says yes, the condition limits the divorce. Abu Hanifa says the sentence is one unit that speaks only about the condition and is silent elsewhere—therefore no restriction is implied. The disagreement has practical implications for conditional oaths and divorces.

Guided Path
1
State the Shafi'i position and its linguistic basis
Al-Shafi'i considers the consequence (jaza) as the main speech, and the condition as a restriction (qayd) upon it, similar to an adverbial phrase. Thus, the condition implies that the ruling is restricted to the time of the condition, and by implication, it does not occur outside it.
This is attributed to 'ahl al-'arabiyya' (the people of Arabic grammar).
2
State the Hanafi position and its rational basis
Abu Hanifa treats the entire conditional sentence (shart + jaza) as one integrated speech unit. It indicates the occurrence of the ruling at the condition but is silent about other times. Therefore, it does not imply restriction or negation elsewhere.
This is called 'madhhab ahl al-ma'qul' (the school of rationalists).
3
Analyze the dispute over attribution to Arabic grammarians
Some scholars claim that the Shafi'i view is the position of Arabic grammarians. However, the text notes that this attribution is disputed; the grammarians actually say the ruling is 'between the condition and the consequence,' implying the whole is one unit.
The commentator (Al-Qamar) quotes that the grammarians did not say the consequence alone is speech.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In cases where a condition is not fulfilled, the Shafi'i view would deduce that the ruling is negated (by mafhum al-mukhalafa). The Hanafi view does not deduce negation; it only says the ruling is not actualized because the condition was not met, but it does not imply the ruling would not occur in another context.
Example: 'If you enter, you are divorced.' Wife does not enter. Shafi'i: she is not divorced, and the condition implies she would not be divorced for any other act. Hanafi: she is not divorced because condition not met, but the statement does not address other acts.
5
Understand the Hanafi response regarding mafhum al-mukhalafa
The Hanafi school rejects the application of mafhum al-mukhalafa to conditional clauses. The text argues that conditional sentences do not by language or logic entail negation when the condition is absent.
This is consistent with the Hanafi position that mafhum al-mukhalafa is limited to specific contexts like descriptions.

بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة فتقبل التعليق بكماله بخلاف البيع؛ فإنّه من قبيل الإثباتات ولا يقبل التعليق؛ إذ به يصير قمارًا. فإذا دخل عليه خيار الشرط، يكون مانعًا للحكم فقط دون السبب؛ ليقل أثر الشرط حتى الإمكان. وقد يقرّر الاختلاف بيننا وبينه بعنوان آخر، وهو أن الشافعي يقول: إنّ الكلام هو الجزاء، والشرط قيد له، فكأنه قال: أنت طالق في وقت دخولكِ الدّارَ، فهذا القيد يفيد حصر الطلاق فيه، وهو مذهب أهل العربية، وأبو حنيفة لله يقول: إن الجزاء كلاهما بمنزلة كلام واحد يدلّ على وقوع الطلاق حين الشرط، وساكت عن سائر التقادير، فلا يدل على الحصر، وهو مذهب أهل المعقول. ولم يذكر المصنف مي جوابًا عن الوصف، إمّا لأنّ الجواب عن الشرط جواب عنه، وإما لوضوحه وشهرته، = الشرط فيه، إلا أنه شرع على خلاف القياس للضرورة؛ فيكون داخلاً في الحكم فقط دون السبب، لارتفاع الضرورة حينئذ، والضرورة تقدّر بقدرها؛ ولأنه لو دخل في السبب والحكم جميعًا، لبطل معنى الإثبات ورعايته بالكلية، ولو لم يدخل فيهما لم يبق أثر الشرط أصلاً؛ فصرنا إلى ما ذكرنا رعاية للجانبين.(السنبلي) التعليق بكماله : أي التعليق الكامل، وهو تعليق السبب والحكم جميعًا. (القمر) من قبيل الإثباتات: فإنه يثبت الملك. إذ به: أي بالتعليق والخطر والشرط يصير البيع قمارًا وهو حرام (القمر) مانعا للحكم: فقط فإنّ القياس أن لا يجوز البيع مع خيار الشرط كما لا يجوز بشروط أخر، إلّا أنّ الشرع جوّز ذلك ضرورةً دفع الغبن؛ فيتقدّر بقدر الضرورة، وهي تندفع بجعل الشرط مانعا لحكم البيع وهو الملك دون السبب وهو البيع؛ لئلا يلغو الشرط ويقل الخطر مع حصول المقصود وهو دفع الغبن؛ فإنه يمكن لصاحب الخيار فسخ البيع. (القمر) دون السبب ولذا إذا حلف لا يبيع، فباع بشرط الخيار يحنث؛ لأنّ شرط الخيار ليس بمانع للسبب؛ فيتحقق البيع. (القمر) وقد يقرّر إلخ : المقرّر صاحب "التلويح" . (القمر) قيد له أي بمنزلة الظرف أو الحال.(القمر) يفيد حصر إلخ فالقيد مخصص، فيلزم نفي الحكم عند عدم هذا القيد أي الشرط. (القمر) مذهب أهل العربية قيل: إنّ هذه النسبة افتراء، فإنّ أهل العربية قالوا: إن الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء، فالمجموع كلام وليس أحد من طرفيه كلامًا، ولم يقولوا إنّ الكلام هو الجزاء، والشرط قيد له، بل إنما قاله صاحب "المفتاح". (القمر) وساكت إلخ : أقول: للخصم أن يقول: إنا سلّمنا أنّ الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء، فالمجموع كلام مفيد لحكم تعليقي بالمنطوق. لكنه لا نسلم أنه ساكت عن سائر التقادير، بل هو عين النزاع؛ فإنا نقول: إنه يدلّ على نفي الحكم عند عدم الشرط بطريق مفهوم المخالفة. (القمر) وهو أي الحكم بين الشرط والجزاء. (القمر) الجواب: جواب عنه أي عن الوصف، لأن الشافعي له ألحق الوصف بالشرط. (القمر)
Statement of the divisions of the fourth division: Section on mentioning the corrupt aspects. It accepts complete suspension (ta'liq kamil) unlike sale; for sale is of the type of property establishment and does not accept suspension; because by it it becomes gambling. When the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) enters it, it prevents only the ruling, not the cause, so that the effect of the condition is minimal as much as possible. And the difference between us and him (al-Shafi'i) is established under another heading, namely that al-Shafi'i says: The speech is the consequence (jaza), and the condition is a restriction (qayd) upon it, as if he said: 'You are divorced at the time of your entering the house.' So this restriction implies the restriction of divorce to it, and this is the school of the people of grammar. And Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, says: The consequence and both (the condition and consequence) are like one single speech indicating the occurrence of divorce at the condition, and silent about other estimations, so it does not indicate restriction, and this is the school of rationalists. And the author did not mention an answer regarding the descriptive phrase (wasf), either because the answer about the condition is an answer about it, or due to its clarity and fame. ... (commentary continues)...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is a passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work discussing a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the effect of conditional clauses (ta'liq) and the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) in contracts like sale and divorce. The author distinguishes between divorce and sale: divorce accepts complete suspension (both cause and ruling are suspended), while sale does not because it would become gambling. However, when khiyar al-shart enters a sale, it only prevents the ruling (i.e., ownership) but not the cause (the contract itself), due to necessity (darura) to prevent disadvantage (ghubn). This is an exception to qiyas. Then the commentator explains the difference between Hanafi and Shafi'i on the semantics of conditional sentences. Al-Shafi'i considers the consequence (jaza) as the main speech and the condition as a restriction, implying that the ruling is confined to the time of the condition. Abu Hanifa treats the entire conditional sentence as a single utterance that is silent about other times, thus it does not imply restriction. The commentator adds that some attribute the Shafi'i view to the Arabic grammarians, but this is disputed. The Hanafi position is that the conditional does not carry a concept of opposition (mafhum al-mukhalafa) that negates the ruling when the condition is absent. This is a fundamental usul dispute rooted in language and logic.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspending the occurrence of a legal ruling on a future condition; here it refers specifically to suspending divorce on a condition, where both the cause and ruling are suspended (ta'liq kamil).
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option in a contract that allows one party to rescind the contract within a specified period; in a sale, it prevents the ruling (ownership) from taking effect immediately but does not affect the cause (the contract).
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafa): The implied meaning that a ruling is negated when the condition or attribute mentioned is absent; the Hanafis reject this implication in conditional statements like 'if...then...'.
الجزاء (al-jaza): The consequence or result clause in a conditional sentence (e.g., 'you are divorced' in 'if you enter, you are divorced').
الشرط (al-shart): The condition clause in a conditional sentence (e.g., 'if you enter').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, a conditional sentence like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' implies:
A) The divorce is restricted to the time of entry, so it does not occur otherwise.
B) The divorce occurs when the condition is met, and the sentence is silent about other times.
C) The divorce occurs when the condition is met, and by implication, it does not occur otherwise.
D) The condition is irrelevant; the divorce is effective regardless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats conditional sentences as a single speech unit that does not imply restriction or negation when the condition is absent; this differs from the Shafi'i view.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

Command and Prohibition: Hanafi Ruling on Their Opposites
Explaining the Hanafi position on command/prohibition implying their opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the implication of command and prohibition for their opposites and explain the reasoning based on the derivative nature of the ruling.

Core Idea
A command implies only dislike of its opposite; a prohibition implies only a stressed sunnah for its opposite.

This lesson addresses a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: does a command imply the prohibition of its opposite? Does a prohibition imply the obligation of its opposite? The Hanafi school adopts a nuanced position, assigning weaker rulings to the opposites because the implication is derivative. Understanding this clarifies how secondary legal implications are derived without overstating the reach of primary commands and prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine exactly what is being asked: what ruling does a command produce for its opposite? What ruling does a prohibition produce for its opposite?
The opposite (al-didd) is an act that cannot coexist with the commanded or prohibited act.
2
State the opposing view
Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and later al-Jassas held that command implies prohibition of its opposite and prohibition implies obligation to perform its opposite.
This view is considered 'invalid' (fasid) in the Hanafi tradition.
3
State the Hanafi position
According to the Hanafi school: command implies only dislike (karahah) of its opposite; prohibition implies that its opposite is a stressed sunnah (sunnah wajibah).
These are weaker than full prohibition or obligation.
4
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning
The thing itself does not indicate its opposite. The ruling on the opposite is a necessary consequence of compliance, not a direct command. Because the entailment is derivative, the ruling is lower in strength.
For a command, avoiding the opposite is necessary but only disliked. For a prohibition, performing the opposite is necessary but only a stressed sunnah.
5
Apply the condition
If engaging in the opposite causes missing the commanded act altogether, then it becomes prohibited by consensus. This exception safeguards the primary obligation.
The Hanafi position assumes the command is for immediate execution (fawr).

فصل في ذكر الوجوه الفاسدة بيان أقسام التقسيم الرابع كما قال زفر والشافعي حمله، وإطلاق الجمع عليهما مسامحة باعتبار ما فوق الواحد، ونحوه: لبسوا ثيابهم وركبوا دوابهم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ الآيةَ على ما تقرر في الفقه. (المائدة: ٦) وقيل: الأمر بالشيء هذا وجه ثامن من الوجوه الفاسدة، وفيه اختلاف كثير، فقيل: لا حكم للأمر والنهي في ضدّهما أصلاً، وقيل له حكم فيه، وهو أن الأمر بالشيء النهي عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يكون أمرًا بضده، فيدل الأمر على تحريم ضده؛ فإن كان له ضدّ واحد فيها، وإن كانت له أضداد يقتضى أي يستلزم ضده، والنهي على وجوب وفي النهي يكفي له الإتيان بواحد من الأضداد غير معين، وهذا هو مختار الجصاص به. وعندنا الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يقتضي أن يكون ضده سنة واجبة؛ وذلك لأن الشيء في نفسه لا يدلّ على ضدّه، وإنما يلزم الحكم في الضد ضرورة الامتثال، فتكفي الدرجة الأدنى في ذلك، وهي الكراهة في الأول؛ لأنها دون التحريم والسنة الواجبة في الثاني؛ لأنها دون الفرض، وليس المراد بالاقتضاء المصطلح السابق بجعل غير المنطوق منطوقا لتصحيح المنطوق، بل إثبات أمر لازم فقط، وهذا إذا لم يلزم من الاشتغال بالضد تفويت المأمور به، فإن لزم منه ذلك يكون حراما بالاتفاق، الاشتغال بالضد تفويت المأمور به أي ضد المأمور به السنة سنة واجبة أي كان مؤكدًا، وإنما أقحم المصنف له لفظ في معنى؛ لأن السنة المؤكدة لا يثبت إلا بالنقل لا بالعقل فكيف يصح أن يقال: إن النهي عن الشيء يقتضي أن يكون ضده سنةً واجبةً. ثم اعلم أن المراد من الواجبة السنة المؤكدة القريبة من الواجب؛ فالمراد بالواجبة الضرورية المؤكدة لا الوجوب الاصطلاحي، فلا يرد أن بين السنة والواجب تضادًا فكيف يكون شيء واحد سنةً و واجبًا؟ (القمر) سنة واجبة إلخ: أي سنة مؤكدة قريبة إلى الواجب. وفي 'القواطع' المسألة مصورة فيما إذا كان الأمر للفور لا التراخي. (السنبلي) لأن الشيء إلخ خلاصة الاستدلال منها أن طلب الوجود بالأمر لا يكون بدون إعدام ضده، فكان اقتضاء؛ لأنه ضروري. ولما كان هذا النهي لا يثبت إلا الكراهة، وأما النهي فلأن المنع الأبلغ بطلب الضد، فكان الأمر ضمنيًا؛ فيثبت به الأقل من الواجب . (السنبلي وليس إلخ إذ ليس صحة المنطوق موقوفة عليه. (القمر) بل إثبات إلخ أي بل المراد إثبات أمر لازم؛ فإن الأمر لوجوب إتيان المأمور به فهو ضروري الإتيان، والكفّ عن ضده من لوازم إتيان المأمور به ولما كان الملزوم واجبًا، فاللازم أيضًا واجب فصار هذا الكف واجبًا وصار إتيان ضده حراما، ولما كان حرمة ضده بالتبع وما بالتبع أنزل من الحرمة الأصلية فانحطت رتبتها، وسميت بالكراهة، وكذا النهي لحرمة المنهي عنه، فهو ضروري الكفّ، والاشتغال بضدّه من لوازم الكف عنه، وبضرورة الملزوم يلزم ضرورة اللازم، فصار الاشتغال بضده ضروريًا، ولما كان ضرورة هذا الاشتغال بالتبع، وما بالتبع أنزل من الوجوب الأصلي فانحطت رتبتها وسميت بالسنة الواجبة و لمانع أن يمنع كون الاشتغال بالضد من لوازم الكفّ عنه فإن الكف عنه قد يتحقق بعدم تعلّق الإرادة به وليس ههنا اشتغال بالضد؛ فإنه فعل اختياري، والفعل الاختياري لا يتحقق بدون الإرادة فتأمل (القمر) وهذا أي كراهة ضد المأمور به. (القمر) وهذا إذا لم يلزم إلخ: هذا دفع دخل مقدر تقريره أن ترك صلاة الفرض يُعاقب عليه، والمكروه لا يعاقب عليه؟ وتقرير الدفع أن الكراهة فيما إذا لم يُفوّت الاشتغال به للمأمور به وإن فوّت حرم، وهو المقصود من قول المصنف له فيما بعد وفائدة إلخ. (السنبلي)
Chapter on mentioning the invalid views. Explanation of the divisions of the fourth division: as Zufar and al-Shafi'i said, its application and using the plural for them is a leniency considering what is above one, and similar: 'They wore their clothes and rode their mounts,' and the verse: 'So wash your faces...' as established in fiqh. And it is said: The command for something – this is the eighth invalid view, and there is much disagreement. Some said: There is no ruling for command and prohibition concerning their opposites at all. And some said: There is a ruling for each concerning its opposite, which is that the command for something is a prohibition of its opposite, and the prohibition of something is a command for its opposite. Thus the command indicates the prohibition of its opposite; if it has one opposite, it indicates it; and if it has many opposites, it necessitates (i.e. entails) its opposite. And the prohibition indicates obligation, and in prohibition it suffices to perform one unspecified opposite. This is the choice of al-Jassas. In our view, the command for something entails dislike (karahah) of its opposite, and the prohibition of something entails that its opposite be a stressed sunnah (sunnah wajibah). This is because a thing itself does not indicate its opposite; rather, the ruling concerning the opposite is necessitated by the necessity of compliance. So the lower degree suffices: dislike in the first, because it is below prohibition; and stressed sunnah in the second, because it is below obligation. And what is meant by 'entailment' is not the technical term used earlier (making the unspoken spoken to correct the spoken), but rather establishing a necessary consequence only. This applies when engaging in the opposite does not lead to missing the commanded act. If it leads to missing it, then it is prohibited by consensus. Engaging in the opposite that leads to missing the commanded act, i.e., the opposite of the commanded act. The stressed sunnah is emphasized, and the author inserted the phrase 'in the sense of' because the emphasized sunnah is only established by transmission, not by reason. So how can it be correct to say that prohibition of something entails that its opposite be a stressed sunnah? Then know that what is meant by 'obligatory' is the emphasized sunnah close to obligatory; so what is meant by 'obligatory' is the necessary emphasized, not the technical obligation. Thus it is not objected that there is contradiction between sunnah and obligatory, so how can one thing be both sunnah and obligatory? (al-Qamar) 'stressed sunnah' etc.: i.e., an emphasized sunnah close to obligatory. In al-Qawati', the issue is framed when the command is for immediate execution, not for delay. (al-Sanbali) 'Because the thing' etc.: The summary of the inference is that the demand for existence by command cannot be without eliminating its opposite, so it is an entailment because necessary. Since this prohibition only establishes dislike. As for prohibition, because the stronger prevention is by demanding the opposite, so the command is implicit; thus the lesser of obligation is established. (al-Sanbali) 'And not' etc.: Since the validity of the spoken is not dependent on it. (al-Qamar) 'But establishing' etc.: Rather, what is meant is establishing a necessary consequence. For the command is for the obligation of performing the commanded act, so performance is necessary, and refraining from its opposite is among the necessary consequences of performing the commanded act. Since the primary is obligatory, the necessary also becomes obligatory, so this refraining becomes obligatory, and performing its opposite becomes prohibited. Since the prohibition of the opposite is derivative, and what is derivative is lower than the primary prohibition, its rank lowers and is called dislike. Similarly, prohibition: due to the prohibition of the prohibited thing, refraining is necessary, and engaging in its opposite is among the necessary consequences of refraining from it. By necessity of the primary, the necessary follows; so engagement in its opposite becomes necessary. Since this necessity is derivative, and what is derivative is lower than the primary obligation, its rank lowers and is called stressed sunnah. One might object that engagement in the opposite is not among the necessary consequences of refraining, because refraining may occur by not attaching will to it, and there is no engagement in the opposite here; for it is a voluntary action, and voluntary action does not occur without will. So reflect. (al-Qamar) 'This' refers to dislike of the opposite of the commanded act. (al-Qamar) 'This is when it does not lead' etc.: This is a rebuttal to a hypothetical objection: abandoning the obligatory prayer is punishable, while the disliked is not punishable. The rebuttal: dislike applies when engaging in it does not cause missing the commanded act; if it does cause missing, it is prohibited. That is what the author means later by 'and the benefit' etc. (al-Sanbali)
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of an eighth invalid view in usul al-fiqh concerning the implication of command and prohibition for their opposites. The author first presents the position of Zufar and al-Shafi'i that the command for something implies the prohibition of its opposite, and if there are multiple opposites, all are prohibited. Prohibition implies the obligation to do one unspecified opposite. This view is attributed to al-Jassas as his chosen position. Then the text states the Hanafi position (عندنا): a command implies only dislike (karahah) of its opposite, and a prohibition implies that its opposite is a stressed sunnah (sunnah wajibah). The reasoning is that the thing itself does not indicate its opposite; rather, the ruling on the opposite is a necessary consequence of compliance with the primary command or prohibition. To fulfill the primary, one must avoid its opposite, but this necessity does not rise to the level of the primary ruling. Hence, for a command, the avoidance of the opposite is merely disliked (makruh) rather than prohibited (haram), because it is a lower degree. For a prohibition, engaging in the opposite is necessary for compliance, but since this necessity is derivative, it is a stressed sunnah (sunnah wajibah) rather than a full obligation (fard). The author clarifies that 'entailment' here means a necessary consequence, not the technical term 'iqtiḍā' used in other contexts. An important condition: if engaging in the opposite actually leads to missing the commanded act entirely, then it becomes prohibited by consensus. The text also addresses an objection: how can reason establish a stressed sunnah when stressed sunnah usually requires textual basis? The response is that this is a metaphorical use of 'obligatory' meaning an emphasized sunnah close to obligation, not technical wajib. The commentary further explains that the command for immediate execution (الفور) is assumed; if for delay (التراخي), the analysis may differ. The overall message is that the Hanafi school avoids making extreme implications from command and prohibition, recognizing the derivative nature of rulings on opposites.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr): A speech demanding an action from a superior to an inferior requiring compliance.
النهي (al-nahy): A speech demanding cessation of an action from a superior to an inferior.
الضد (al-didd): The opposite of a commanded or prohibited act; that which cannot coexist with the primary act.
الكراهة (al-karahah): Dislike; a ruling that the act is blameworthy but not sinful or punishable.
السنة الواجبة (al-sunnah al-wajibah): Stressed sunnah; an emphasized recommended act, lower than obligatory (fard) but higher than ordinary sunnah.
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Necessary consequence or entailment, not the technical 'making the unspoken spoken'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on the opposite of a commanded act?
A) It is prohibited (haram).
B) It is disliked (makruh).
C) It is obligatory (wajib).
D) It is a stressed sunnah (sunnah wajibah).

Key Takeaway
A command implies only dislike of its opposite; a prohibition implies only a stressed sunnah for its opposite — the rulings are derivative and therefore weaker.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Prohibition Entails Its Opposite as Sunnah Wajibah: The Sajdah Ikhtilaf
Explaining the usul rule that prohibition entails a wajib-like opposite and the sajdah ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that prohibition (nahy) entails a wajib-like opposite (sunnah wajibah) and evaluate the internal Hanafi dispute on prostration on impure ground using qiyas.

Core Idea
A prohibition forces its opposite into a wajib sunnah status — but rationally, not by transmission.

This passage explores a cornerstone of Hanafi usul: when something is prohibited, its necessary opposite takes on a force similar to a confirmed sunnah. The commentator applies this to the muhrim's garments and then to a heated debate about praying on impure ground, where Abu Yusuf parts ways with the two Imams. Understanding this derivation illuminates how Hanafis blend rational inference with textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle and its application
State the rule: prohibition entails that its opposite is like a sunnah wajibah. Apply it to the muhrim prohibited from stitched garments — the opposite (izār and ridā') becomes required.
The opposite must be the minimal sufficient alternative (e.g., covering nakedness).
2
Distinguish sunnah wajibah from sunnah istilahiyyah
The commentator clarifies that this derived opposite is not a transmitted sunnah but a rational necessity. Terminological sunnah is only from the Prophet (saw).
This is a key Hanafi nuance — not all obligations are transmitted.
3
Analyze Abu Yusuf's position on sajdah on najis
Abu Yusuf argues prayer is not invalidated because the command is to prostrate on pure; prostration on impure is not prohibited and does not permanently nullify the command.
He relies on the principle that the opposite does not void the commanded act if it can be redone.
4
Analyze the two Imams' counterposition
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad analogize to fasting: purity is a perpetual fard in prayer; prostration on najis violates it just as eating violates fasting. Thus prayer is invalid.
This is qiyas: the common illa is the violation of a continuous obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: according to Abu Yusuf, prayer is valid (though makruh); according to the Imams, it is invalid and must be repeated. The mufta bihi is the Imams' view.
The majority view prevails, and the text implies this is the relied-upon position.

Explanation of the sections of the fourth division: Chapter on mentioning invalid aspects. In the restricted time, it is prohibited even if that opposite is in itself an intended worship or a permissible matter. This is why we said: When the muhrim was prohibited from wearing stitched garments, it became from the sunnah to wear the izār and ridā', a branch (meaning other than the two threads) based on the principle that prohibition entails that its opposite is in the meaning of an obligatory sunnah. That is because when the muhrim was prohibited from wearing stitched garments, he must wear something to cover the nakedness, and the minimum sufficiency is the izār and ridā', so they must not be abandoned just as the confirmed sunnah is not abandoned. Otherwise, the terminological sunnah is what is narrated from the Messenger of Allah, say or deed, not what is established by reason. Abu Yusuf said: its place is as a conjunction to his statement 'we said', and a branch on the principle that command entails dislike of its opposite in other than order of wording, meaning for this rule Abu Yusuf said especially: Whoever prostrates on an impure place, his prayer is not invalidated because it is not intended by the prohibition; rather what is commanded is the act of prostration on a pure place. So if he repeats it on a pure place, it is permissible according to him, so engagement in prostration on (impure) is prohibited because it nullifies the prayer. (al-Qamar:) 'sufficiency' etc. meaning in covering the nakedness and protection from heat and cold. When the prohibition of stitched garments occurred, its opposite became from the obligatory sunnah in al-Hidāyah: wear two new or washed garments, izār and ridā', because he tied and wore a loincloth and mantle at his iḥrām. (al-Qamar:) 'just as the confirmed sunnah is not abandoned' etc. Contains indication that prohibition of a thing entails that its opposite is agreed upon from the confirmed sunnah, it does not entail that its opposite is a confirmed sunnah, otherwise it would not be straight; for the terminological sunnah etc. (al-Qamar:) 'otherwise' etc. meaning even if the meaning of the opposite being in the meaning of the mentioned sunnah does not necessitate its non-abandonment, there is no way for it to be a confirmed sunnah at all because the terminological sunnah etc. (al-Sanablī:) 'entails' i.e. when the opposite does not nullify what is commanded. (al-Qamar:) 'in other than order of wording' and when the branch of the origin of prohibition from our scholars, he advanced it, and the branch of the origin of command according to Abū Yūsuf was only for him, not for both parties, so he delayed it. (al-Qamar:) 'because' etc. meaning because prostration on an impure place is not intended by the prohibition, for the prohibition was not explicitly mentioned about prostration on the impure place (al-Qamar:) 'on a pure place' due to the establishment of consensus that what is intended by His statement, Exalted, 'and prostrate' (Hajj 77) is prostration on the pure place, as in some commentaries. Purification from carrying najis is a perpetual obligation, so its opposite becomes nullifying of the obligation as in fasting. Just as abstaining from fulfilling desire is an obligation in fasting, and fasting is nullified by eating in a part of its time, likewise abstaining from carrying najis is an obligation in prayer, and it is nullified by prostration on an impure place, so it invalidates.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on the usul principle that prohibition (nahy) entails that its opposite (didd) takes the status of an obligatory sunnah (sunnah wajibah) in terms of being required to be performed. The commentator begins by stating the rule: in a restricted time, the opposite of a prohibited act is prohibited even if that opposite is itself an act of worship or permissible. Then he applies it to the muhrim (person in ihram) who is prohibited from wearing stitched garments. Necessarily, he must cover his nakedness; thus wearing an izār (waist-wrapper) and radā' (mantle) becomes like a sunnah wajibah that must not be abandoned. However, he clarifies that this does not make it a terminological sunnah (sunnah istilāhiyyah), which is only established by transmission from the Prophet. The discussion then shifts to a branch: Abu Yusuf's opinion on praying on impure ground. Abu Yusuf says that if one prostrates on an impure place, the prayer is not invalidated because the act of prostration is not prohibited per se; the command is to prostrate on a pure place. If one repeats the prostration on a pure place, the prayer becomes valid. His reasoning is that the opposite (prostration on impure) does not nullify the commanded act (prostration on pure) because the prayer can be completed. The two Imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree: they analogize to fasting, where abstaining from desire is a perpetual obligation. In prayer, purity from najis is a continuous obligation. Prostrating on najis makes the face a carrier of najis, violating that obligation, and thus invalidates the prayer, just as eating invalidates the fast. The passage concludes by noting that the author transitioned to the next topic after finishing the discussion of the book's divisions.

Key Terms
النهي يقتضي (al-nahy yaqtadī): The principle that a prohibition in Islamic law inherently requires that the opposite of the prohibited act takes a legal status of being necessary (like a sunnah wajibah) to fulfill the intent of the law.
السنة الواجبة (al-sunnah al-wājibah): A level of legal obligation derived from the opposite of a prohibition; not a transmitted sunnah but a rational necessity to avoid the prohibited. It is required but not part of the technical sunnah.
السنة الاصطلاحية (al-sunnah al-iṣṭilāḥiyyah): The technical definition of sunnah: what is transmitted from the Prophet (sayings or deeds) that is recommended but not obligatory.
المخيط (al-makhīṭ): Sewn or stitched garments; prohibited for the muhrim (person in ihram) during Hajj/Umrah.
الإزار والرداء (al-izār wa al-ridā’): Two unstitched garments: the izār (waist-wrapper) and the ridā’ (mantle worn over the shoulders). These are the required garments for the muhrim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the two Imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why does prostration on an impure place invalidate the prayer?
A) Because the act of prostration is explicitly prohibited in the Quran.
B) Because it causes the face to carry najis, violating the perpetual obligation of purity in prayer, analogous to eating invalidating fasting.
C) Because the muhrim is prohibited from wearing stitched garments.
D) Because Abu Yusuf said so.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that prohibition entails its opposite as a sunnah wajibah is rational, not transmitted, and its application to sajdah on najis reveals an internal ikhtilaf based on whether the opposite nullifies the commanded act.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Rukhsah: The Hanafi Principle of Concession Under Necessity
Explanation of rukhsah with examples from ikrah and idtirar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of rukhsah in Hanafi usul al-fiqh and apply the principle of 'loss of personal right compensated by preservation of divine or third-party rights' to new cases of coercion or necessity.

Core Idea
Rukhsah: your life is lost if you refuse, but Allah's right remains through compensation.

In this passage from Noor al-Anwar, the author presents six concrete examples demonstrating when a prohibited act becomes permissible under coercion or dire need. These examples illustrate a core principle of Hanafi usul: the right of the individual (life or limb) takes precedence over the strict prohibition when the divine or interpersonal right can be fulfilled later through compensation. Understanding this principle allows you to distinguish true rukhsah from mere license and to correctly apply it in situations of genuine necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common pattern in each example
Read each example (utterance of kufr, breaking fast, destroying property, leaving amr bil ma'ruf, violating ihram, consuming property of another). For each, note that the prohibited act and its sanctity both remain in principle, but permission is granted because the person's own right (life or limb) is entirely lost upon refusal, while the right of Allah or others is preserved through a substitute (qada', daman, ghurm, etc.)
The pattern is: haqq al-nafs lost entirely → haqq Allah or haqq al-ghayr can be compensated later.
2
Extract the legal ratio (talil)
The reasoning (talil) is that the preservation of life and limb (darurah) overrides the immediate obligation when compensation is possible. This is the 'illa for rukhsah: the right of the individual (haqq al-nafs) takes precedence over the strict ruling of prohibition when the other rights can be fulfilled later.
This is not an abrogation (naskh) but a temporary concession (rukhsah).
3
Apply the rule to a hypothetical
Consider a person forced at gunpoint to drink wine. Using the pattern: his life is at risk (right lost entirely), and the right of Allah (prohibition of wine) can be compensated by repentance and istighfar. Therefore, it is permitted as a rukhsah, provided he dislikes the act in his heart and does not consent.
The passage explicitly conditions rukhsah on inner disapproval (being 'karaha' in heart).
4
Note the condition of inner disapproval
The passage states that for the person who leaves amr bil ma'ruf out of fear, it is permissible only 'on condition that he dislikes it in his heart.' This condition applies to all rukhsah cases: the person must not consent to the act but only perform it under duress.
If one consents or willingly performs the act, it is not rukhsah but a sin.
5
Contrast with azimah (strict ruling)
Rukhsah does not cancel the original prohibition. The prohibition remains in principle (azimah). The concession is a temporary allowance for necessity. For example, the prohibition of hunting for a muhrim remains, but if coerced, one may hunt, although he must still pay the penalty (jaza') as compensation.
Acting by azimah (refraining even under threat) is rewarded more, as the passage says 'patience is better.'
